                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2019 51:9"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="We report the first annotated chromosome-level reference genome assembly for pea, Gregor Mendel&#8217;s original genetic model. Phylogenetics and paleogenomics show genomic rearrangements across legumes and suggest a major role for repetitive elements in pea genome evolution. Compared to other sequenced Leguminosae genomes, the pea genome shows intense gene dynamics, most likely associated with genome size expansion when the Fabeae diverged from its sister tribes. During Pisum evolution, translocation and transposition differentially occurred across lineages. This reference sequence will accelerate our understanding of the molecular basis of agronomically important traits and support crop improvement. The first annotated chromosome-level reference genome assembly for pea, Gregor Mendel&#8217;s original genetic model, provides insights into legume genome evolution and the molecular basis of agricultural traits for pea improvement."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-2942-1098"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-2942-1098</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff11">11</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="UMR 1095 Génétique, Diversité, Ecophysiologie des Céréales, INRA, Université Clermont Auvergne" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000000115480420, grid.494717.8, UMR 1095 Génétique, Diversité, Ecophysiologie des Céréales, INRA, Université Clermont Auvergne, Clermont-Ferrand, France" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-David-Edwards" data-author-popup="auth-David-Edwards">David Edwards</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-7599-6760"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-7599-6760</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="School of Biological Sciences and Institute of Agriculture, University of Western Australia" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 7910, grid.1012.2, School of Biological Sciences and Institute of Agriculture, University of Western Australia, Perth, Western Australia, Australia" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Judith-Lichtenzveig" data-author-popup="auth-Judith-Lichtenzveig">Judith Lichtenzveig</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8817-5988"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-8817-5988</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff18">18</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="School of Agriculture and Environment, University of Western Australia" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 7910, grid.1012.2, School of Agriculture and Environment, University of Western Australia, Perth, Western Australia, Australia" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Ji__-Macas" data-author-popup="auth-Ji__-Macas">Jiří Macas</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-0829-1570"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-0829-1570</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Biology Centre, Czech Academy of Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2255 8513, grid.418338.5, Biology Centre, Czech Academy of Sciences, České Budějovice, Czech Republic" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jaroslav-Dole_el" data-author-popup="auth-Jaroslav-Dole_el">Jaroslav Doležel</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-6263-0492"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-6263-0492</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Institute of Experimental Botany, Centre of the Region Haná for Biotechnological and Agricultural Research" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.454748.e, Institute of Experimental Botany, Centre of the Region Haná for Biotechnological and Agricultural Research, Olomouc, Czech Republic" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Patrick-Wincker" data-author-popup="auth-Patrick-Wincker">Patrick Wincker</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Génomique Métabolique, Genoscope, Institut François Jacob, CEA, CNRS, Université Evry, Université Paris-Saclay" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0641 2997, grid.434728.e, Génomique Métabolique, Genoscope, Institut François Jacob, CEA, CNRS, Université Evry, Université Paris-Saclay, Evry, France" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Judith-Burstin" data-author-popup="auth-Judith-Burstin" data-corresp-id="c1">Judith Burstin<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-4125-3499"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-4125-3499</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Agroécologie, AgroSup Dijon, INRA, Université Bourgogne Franche-Comté Bourgogne, Université Bourgogne Franche-Comté" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0445 7139, grid.462299.2, Agroécologie, AgroSup Dijon, INRA, Université Bourgogne Franche-Comté Bourgogne, Université Bourgogne Franche-Comté, Dijon, France" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>We report the first annotated chromosome-level reference genome assembly for pea, Gregor Mendel’s original genetic model. Phylogenetics and paleogenomics show genomic rearrangements across legumes and suggest a major role for repetitive elements in pea genome evolution. Compared to other sequenced Leguminosae genomes, the pea genome shows intense gene dynamics, most likely associated with genome size expansion when the Fabeae diverged from its sister tribes. During <i>Pisum</i> evolution, translocation and transposition differentially occurred across lineages. This reference sequence will accelerate our understanding of the molecular basis of agronomically important traits and support crop improvement.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Pea (<i>Pisum sativum</i> L., 2<i>n</i> = 14) is the second most important grain legume in the world after common bean and is an important green vegetable with 14.3 t of dry pea and 19.9 t of green pea produced in 2016 (<a href="http://www.fao.org/faostat/">http://www.fao.org/faostat/</a>). Pea belongs to the Leguminosae (or Fabaceae), which includes cool season grain legumes from the Galegoid clade, such as pea, lentil (<i>Lens culinaris</i> Medik.), chickpea (<i>Cicer arietinum</i> L.), faba bean (<i>Vicia faba</i> L.) and tropical grain legumes from the Milletoid clade, such as common bean (<i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i> L.), cowpea (<i>Vigna unguiculata</i> (L.) Walp.) and mungbean (<i>Vigna radiata</i> (L.) R. Wilczek). It provides significant ecosystem services: it is a valuable source of dietary proteins, mineral nutrients, complex starch and fibers with demonstrated health benefits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Burstin, J., Gallardo, K., Mir, R. R., Varshney, R. K. &amp; Duc, G. Improving protein content and nutrition quality, in Biology and Breeding of Food Legumes (eds Pratap, A. &amp; Kumar, J.) 314–328 (CAB International, 2011)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1186">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Guillon, F. &amp; Champ, M. M.-J. Carbohydrate fractions of legumes: uses in human nutrition and potential for health. Br. J. Nutr. 88, S293–S306 (2002)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1186_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dahl, W. J., Foster, L. M. &amp; Tyler, R. T. Review of the health benefits of peas (Pisum sativum L.). Br. J. Nutr. 108, S3–S10 (2012)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1186_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Foschia, M., Horstmann, S. W., Arendt, E. K. &amp; Zannini, E. Legumes as functional ingredients in gluten-free bakery and pasta products. Ann. Rev. Food Sci. Technol. 8, 75–96 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1189">4</a></sup> and its symbiosis with N-fixing soil bacteria reduces the need for applied N fertilizers so mitigating greenhouse gas emissions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nemecek, T. et al. Environmental impacts of introducing grain legumes into European crop rotations. Eur. J. Agron. 28, 380–393 (2008)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1193">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Crews, T. E. &amp; Peoples, M. B. Legume versus fertilizer sources of nitrogen: ecological tradeoffs and human needs. Agric. Ecosyst. Environ. 102, 279–297 (2004)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1193_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Poore, J. &amp; Nemecek, T. Reducing food’s environmental impacts through producers and consumers. Science 360, 987–992 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1196">7</a></sup>. Pea was domesticated ~10,000 years ago by Neolithic farmers of the Fertile Crescent, along with cereals and other grain legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zohary, D. &amp; Hopf, M. Domestication of Plants in the Old World (Oxford Univ. Press, Oxford, 2000)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1201">8</a></sup>. The large reservoir of genetic diversity in <i>Pisum</i> has facilitated its spread throughout Asia, Europe, Africa, the Americas and Oceania where it has adapted to diverse environments and culinary practices (<a href="https://iyp2016.org/">https://iyp2016.org/</a>). Due to its large genome size (1 C ~ 4.45 gigabases, Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Doležel, J. et al. Plant genome size estimation by flow cytometry: inter-laboratory comparison. Ann. Bot. 82, 17–26 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1215">9</a></sup>), pea genomics has lagged behind that of legumes with smaller genomes, such as <i>Medicago truncatula</i> Gaertn.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1222">10</a></sup>, <i>Lotus japonicus</i> L.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227–239 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1230">11</a></sup> or soybean (<i>Glycine max</i> (L.) Merr)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1237">12</a></sup>. Yet, pea has been studied as a genetic model since the eighteenth century; the analysis of the inheritance of different pea morphotypes led Gregor Mendel to uncover the laws of genetics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Mendel, G. Versuche über Pflanzenhybriden. Verhandlungen des naturforschenden Vereines in Brünn, Bd. IV für das Jahr 1865. Abhandlungen, 3–47 (1866)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1241">13</a></sup>. Several pea developmental mutations have since been characterized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ellis, T. H. N., Hofer, J. M. I., Timmerman-Vaughan, G. M., Coyne, C. J. &amp; Hellens, R. P. Mendel, 150 years on. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 590–596 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1245">14</a></sup> and chromosomal regions controlling agronomic traits identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Tayeh, N. et al. Genomic tools in pea breeding programs: status and perspectives. Front. Plant Sci. 6, 1037 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1250">15</a></sup>, but tools exploiting pea diversity for plant breeding, identifying favorable alleles underlying phenotypic variations and accelerating trait improvement by marker-assisted selection have been limited. The pea genome is large, probably resulting from a recent expansion and diversification of retrotransposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ellis, T. H. N. &amp; Poyser, S. J. An integrated and comparative view of pea genetic and cytogenetic maps. New Phytol. 153, 17–25 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1254">16</a></sup>. Early reassociation kinetic studies of the pea genome indicated that 75–97% is made up of a heterogeneous population of repetitive sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Flavell, R. B., Bennett, M. D., Smith, J. B. &amp; Smith, D. B. Genome size and the proportion of repeated nucleotide sequence DNA in plants. Biochem. Genet. 12, 257–269 (1974)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1258">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Murray, M. G., Peters, D. L. &amp; Thompson, W. F. Ancient repeated sequences in the pea and mung bean genomes and implications for genome evolution. J. Mol. Evol. 17, 31–42 (1981)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1261">18</a></sup>. More recent investigations confirmed the occurrence of highly diverse families of high to moderately repeated sequences comprising about 76% of pea nuclear DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Macas, J. et al. In depth characterization of repetitive DNA in 23 plant genomes reveals sources of genome size variation in the legume tribe Fabeae. PLoS One 10, e0143424 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1265">19</a></sup>. When the repetitive DNA sequences of pea, soybean and <i>M. truncatula</i> are compared, little sequence similarity is found between pea and soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Macas, J. et al. In depth characterization of repetitive DNA in 23 plant genomes reveals sources of genome size variation in the legume tribe Fabeae. PLoS One 10, e0143424 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1272">19</a></sup>. Repetitive sequences between pea and <i>M. truncatula</i> were more similar but differed in abundance. The pea karyotype includes two sub-metacentric (1 and 2) and five acrocentric (3, 4, 5, 6 and 7) chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ellis, T. H. N. &amp; Poyser, S. J. An integrated and comparative view of pea genetic and cytogenetic maps. New Phytol. 153, 17–25 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1280">16</a></sup>. Several major rearrangements, including translocations between nonhomologous chromosomes, have been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hammarlund, C. &amp; Håkansson, A. Parallelism of chromosome ring formation, sterility and linkage in Pisum. Hereditas 14, 97–98 (1930)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1284">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sansome, E. Segmental interchange lines in Pisum sativum. Nature 139, 113 (1937)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1284_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Lamm, R. &amp; Miravalle, R. J. A translocation tester set in Pisum. Hereditas 45, 417–440 (1959)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1287">22</a></sup>.</p><p>Technological innovation now enables the sequencing and assembly of large genomes, bridging the gap between models and crops for quantitative trait analysis and genome-wide breeding approaches. Accordingly, an international consortium was formed to produce a reference genome sequence for pea. Here we report the draft assembly of the seven chromosomes of the inbred pea cultivar ‘Caméor’, released by the French breeding company Seminor in 1973 and characterized by its protein-rich seeds. This fully annotated assembly builds on genomic resources developed for Caméor over the last decade (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">1</a>) and will enable genomic-assisted crop improvement. It provides insights into legume genome evolution, with resequencing data for 42 wild, landrace and cultivar <i>Pisum</i> genotypes, revealing genomic events that have shaped the evolution of this large and diverse genus.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Complementary approaches were combined to obtain the pea reference genome assembly (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">2</a>). Whole-genome Illumina short-read sequences (281× genome coverage; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">1</a>) were assembled into contigs using SoapdeNovo2, then combined into scaffolds using long-range PacBio RSII sequences (13× genome coverage; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">1</a>) and whole-genome profiling of a bacterial articial chromosome (BAC) library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Gali, K. K. et al. Development of a sequence-based reference physical map of pea (Pisum sativum L.). Front. Plant Sci. 10, 323 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1321">23</a></sup>. Scaffolds were manually curated for inter and intrachromosomal chimeras using (1) sequences obtained from single chromosomes isolated by flow-cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Neumann, P., Pozárková, D., Vrána, J., Doležel, J. &amp; Macas, J. Chromosome sorting and PCR-based physical mapping in pea (Pisum sativum L.). Chromosome Res. 10, 63–71 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1325">24</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">3</a>) and (2) an ultra-high-density skim genotyping-by-sequencing genetic map (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">1</a>). Curated scaffolds were then integrated into 24,623 super-scaffolds (L50 of 415 kilobases (kb), Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">2</a>) using BioNano maps (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">4</a>). The seven pseudomolecules representing the pea chromosomes were obtained by anchoring super-scaffolds onto high-density genetic maps (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM4">2</a>). Pseudomolecules were named according to the reference pea genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tayeh, N. et al. Development of two major resources for pea genomics: the GenoPea 13.2K SNP Array and a high density, high resolution consensus genetic map. Plant J. 84, 1257–1273 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1349">25</a></sup> and chromosome numbering<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Neumann, P., Pozárková, D., Vrána, J., Doležel, J. &amp; Macas, J. Chromosome sorting and PCR-based physical mapping in pea (Pisum sativum L.). Chromosome Res. 10, 63–71 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1353">24</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>The pea genome v.1a assembly spans 3.92 Gb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Tab1">1</a>) representing ~88% of the estimated pea genome size (~4.45 Gb), with 82.5% (3.23 Gb) of sequences assigned to the seven pseudomolecules and 14,266 unassigned scaffolds representing 685 Mb. The estimated size gap between the genome and assembly was mostly due to highly repeated sequences collapsed in the assembly, reflected by repeat proportions in unassembled reads compared to the assembly (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">6</a>). The most under-represented repeats were tandemly arranged satellite repeats and ribosomal RNA genes whose arrays were highly reduced or absent from the assembly, accounting for about 15% of the missing sequence and probably more at the centromeres and telomeres. No group of dispersed repeats was missing from the assembly, but under-representation of high copy number mobile elements accounted for most (~75%) of the difference between assembly length and estimated genome size. Recent long read sequencing technologies should in the future allow access to collapsed repeats and missing sequences.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Characteristics of the pea genome assembly v.1a</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Centromere positions were indicated by regions of suppressed meiotic recombination revealed by comparing marker positions in the skim-GBS genetic map with the pseudomolecules (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">5</a>). These were confirmed using selected sequences for FISH (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig1">1b–f</a>). Pea chromosomes are metapolycentric, characterized by extended primary constrictions containing multiple domains of centromeric histone cenH3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Neumann, P. et al. Stretching the rules: monocentric chromosomes with multiple centromere domains. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002777 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1813">26</a></sup>. The coordinates of nonrecombining regions of the pseudomolecules agreed well with centromere positions obtained from cytogenetic measurements of the pea karyotype (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig1">1b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). Outside centromeres, recombination rate appeared constant along chromosomes and marker order on pseudomolecules was highly (Spearman <i>r</i> &gt; 0.95) collinear with high-density linkage maps of five recombinant inbred line (RIL) populations from intra-specific crosses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tayeh, N. et al. Development of two major resources for pea genomics: the GenoPea 13.2K SNP Array and a high density, high resolution consensus genetic map. Plant J. 84, 1257–1273 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1827">25</a></sup> (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM4">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Pea genome features."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Pea genome features.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="526" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Circos view of the pea genome. Pseudomolecule color-code is shaded at estimated centromere positions. Lanes depict circular representation of pseudomolecules (<b>a</b>) and the density of retrotransposons, transposons, genes, ncRNA, tRNA and miRNA coding sequences (<b>b</b>–<b>g</b>). Lines in the inner circle represent links between synteny-selected paralogs. <b>b</b>, Estimated positions of centromeres in the assembly and their comparison to pea cytogenetic map is schematically represented, with pseudomolecules as white bars and cytogenetic maps of pea chromosomes as gray bars. Non-recombining regions representing the centromeres are marked in green. Positions of centromeric single-copy FISH markers are indicated above the pseudomolecules in black and positions of arrays of centromeric satellites present in the assembly are shown below them in blue. Positions of primary constrictions on the cytogenetic maps are labeled in red. PisTR-B satellite loci used to discriminate individual chromosomes are shown in purple boxes on the gray bars. <b>c</b>, FISH localization of the satellite repeats TR11/19 (red) and TR10 (green) on metaphase chromosomes (gray). <b>d</b>, Discrimination of chromosomes within the pea karyotype using FISH with PisTR-B probe (purple). <b>e</b>, Example of FISH detection of the single-copy marker (1722, green) in the centromere of chromosome 6. <b>f</b>, Chromosome 6 with labeled centromeric repeat TR11/19. <b>g</b>, The density of different TE lineages inferred from the detection of their protein-coding domains along pseudomolecules.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat annotation and gene prediction</h3><p>Annotation (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">6</a>) identified 2,225,175 repetitive elements clustered into 2,940 consensus sequences representing ~83% of the genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Tab1">1</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig1">1a</a>). Most of these corresponded to transposable elements (TE) that were further sub-classified (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">7</a>). Retrotransposons (Class I), with 1,945,520 copies, were the most abundant. Long-terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (1,707,747 copies) represented 72.7% of the genome, with <i>Ty3-gypsy Ogre</i> elements being their major lineage (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">7</a>). The 246,432 transposons (Class II) represented 5.4% of the genome, 84% of which were terminal-inverted repeat (TIR) transposons (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">7</a>). TE family distribution varied across the genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig1">1g</a>). For example, the abundant <i>Ogre</i> family was distributed throughout all chromosomes with a lower density near telomeres. In contrast, <i>Ty1-copia</i> Ivana and <i>Ty3-gypsy</i> TatV were preferentially found near telomeres and <i>Ty3-gypsy</i> chromovirus CRM were mainly located around centromeres.</p><p>Ab initio and homology-based methods were combined to annotate protein-coding sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). In total, 44,756 complete and 29 truncated genes were predicted (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">8</a>), with an average gene length, coding sequence length and exon number of 2,784 base pairs (bp), 1,016 bp and 6.33 exons, respectively. The vast majority of gene models were supported by complementary DNA/expressed sequence tag evidence. The completeness of the gene repertoire was assessed using BUSCO v.3.0.2 (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Sec11">methods</a>). From a core set of 1,440 single-copy ortholog genes from the <i>Embryophyta</i> lineage, 92.3% were complete in the assembly (67.4% as single-copy, 24.9% as duplicates), 2.7% were fragmented and 5.0% were not found, suggesting that the assembly includes most of the pea gene space. We identified 7,191 long non-coding RNAs, 824 transfer RNAs (tRNAs) and 71 microRNAs (miRNAs) expressed in developing seeds (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig1">1a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). Fourteen of these miRNA and their 67 putative targets were identified for the first time (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Legume genome size evolution</h3><p>Genome size varies significantly among land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Pellicer, J., Hidalgo, O., Dodsworth, S. &amp; Leitch, I. Genome size diversity and its impact on the evolution of land plants. Genes 9, 88 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1966">27</a></sup>. The pea genome (~4.45 Gb (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Doležel, J. et al. Plant genome size estimation by flow cytometry: inter-laboratory comparison. Ann. Bot. 82, 17–26 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1970">9</a></sup>)) is within the upper range for the superrosid eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Pellicer, J., Hidalgo, O., Dodsworth, S. &amp; Leitch, I. Genome size diversity and its impact on the evolution of land plants. Genes 9, 88 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1974">27</a></sup>. Among 695 Leguminosae species, only 104 have a larger genome size than <i>P. sativum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bennett, M. C. &amp; Leitch, I. J. Plant DNA C-values Database release 6.0 (FAIRsharing.org, 2012);&#xA;                     https://doi.org/10.25504/FAIRsharing.7qexb2&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1980">28</a></sup>. All but three of these belong to the Fabeae tribe, which includes the genera <i>Lathyrus</i>, <i>Vicia</i>, <i>Pisum</i> and <i>Lens</i>. The Fabeae thus display distinctively large genomes compared to the closely related Trifolieae (genome size ~1.05 Gb) and Cicereae (genome size ~1.27 Gb (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bennett, M. C. &amp; Leitch, I. J. Plant DNA C-values Database release 6.0 (FAIRsharing.org, 2012);&#xA;                     https://doi.org/10.25504/FAIRsharing.7qexb2&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d37755e1997">28</a></sup>)). The pea genome assembly was thus a good opportunity to study the drivers of genome expansion in the Fabeae.</p><p>Genome expansion in plants is primarily driven by polyploidization (whole-genome duplication events) and the proliferation of TEs. A comparison with 21 eudicot species, especially Leguminosae (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">4</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig2">2a,b</a>), showed that pea has an intermediate number of gene-coding sequences (44,791; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">4</a>), ranking fifth after <i>Cajanus cajan</i> (L.) Millsp., <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>Lupinus angustifolius</i> L. and <i>G. max</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig2">2a</a>), the latter two exhibiting recent paleo-polyplodization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2029">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hane, J. K. et al. A comprehensive draft genome sequence for lupin (Lupinus angustifolius), an emerging health food: insights into plant–microbe interactions and legume evolution. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 318–330 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2032">29</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig2">2b</a>). Notably, the pea genome contains the largest percentage of singletons (54%) as compared to other legumes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">5</a>), which could explain why pea was such a successful plant model in early genetics when large collections of mutants were described for contrasting phenotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Blixt, S. Mutation genetics in Pisum. Agric. Hort. Genet. 30, 1–293 (1972)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2043">30</a></sup>. Paralogs and orthologs were identified using Orthofinder (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). The distribution of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) for pea paralog pairs shows no evidence of a recent whole-genome duplication but reflects the ancestral Papilionoideae whole-genome duplication event (PWGD), estimated to have occurred ~55 million years ago (Ma)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2050">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Cannon, S. et al. Multiple polyploidy events in the early radiation of nodulating and nonnodulating legumes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 193–210 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2053">31</a></sup> and the whole-genome triplication event common to the core eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Bowers, J. E., Chapman, B. A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433–438 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2057">32</a></sup>. The pea genome shows the highest whole-genome mutation rate among the Leguminosae, as demonstrated by a shift in the pea PWGD-peak (mode at Ks = 1) compared to other species (for example, <i>M. truncatul</i>a at Ks = 0.83 and <i>G. max</i> at Ks = 0.61; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">9</a>), consistent with pea having the highest percentage of genus specific genes (33%; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">5</a>). We classified paralog pairs according to their presence or absence among taxonomic lineages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig2">2c</a>, Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">5</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">8</a>). About 75% of pea paralogs, specific to <i>Pisum</i> or to the Trifolieae/Fabae clade, show Ks &lt; 0.4, while most specific to inverted-repeat-lacking clade (IRLC) have Ks just below ~0.4 and for the Leguminosae lineages Ks &gt; 0.4 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">8</a>). In sharp contrast, for <i>M. truncatula</i> paralogs, the Ks distribution is higher than in pea, except for those specific to the Leguminosae lineage where Ks is close to the PWGD-peak (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">8</a>). We used synteny as an additional criterion to select a subset of paralog pairs in pea and <i>M. truncatula</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig2">2d</a>). Many of these pea paralogs appeared to be in tandem and have lower Ks (~0.2) than in <i>M. truncatula</i> (Ks ~ 0.5). Gene number, high whole-genome mutation rate, high proportions of recent paralogs and <i>Pisum</i>-specific genes are all indicative of more frequent gene gain or loss in pea, most likely associated with genome size expansion about 24.7 and 17.5 Ma, coincident with the divergence of the Fabeae from its sister tribes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. S. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. F. Evolutionary rates analysis of Leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575–594 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2112">33</a></sup>. The appearance of these paralogs at that time is intriguing and could be related to genome reorganization associated with TE expansion and/or removal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, S. F. et al. Chromosome evolution in connection with repetitive sequences and epigenetics in plants. Genes 8, 290 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2116">34</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Legume phylogenomics."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Legume phylogenomics.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="876" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Number of gene-coding sequences (CDS) against genome size (Mb) for selected Eudicot sequenced genomes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">4</a>). Data points are represented by centered labeled boxes; overlapping points are indicated. <b>b</b>, Maximum likelihood tree calculated using 28 orthologous sequences common to the eudicot species depicted. All clades have 100% support (1,000 bootstrap runs); support is noted otherwise. Branch length represents estimated nucleotide substitutions per site (bar = 0.2 substitutions per site). Whole-genome paleo-polyploidy events are labeled: γ common to all core eudicots, PWGD common to all Papilionoideae within the Leguminosae family; others are lineage-specific (LS): N-LS, S-LS, β and α, SWGD, L-LS, G-LS (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). <b>c</b>, Ks distribution of paralog pairs classified by their lineage specificity: genus specific (white box plots), specific to genera in the Trifolieae-Fabeae clade (blue; paralog pairs common to Psat, Mt, Tpra and Tsub and absent from all other eudicots in the set), the IRLC (green) or the Papilionoideae (orange) clades. Density is denoted both by violin and quartile box-and-whisker plots. Data points are represented by gray jittered circles. Note <i>x</i> axis is presented on a log scale. <b>d</b>, Distribution of pairwise Ks for intra and interchromosomal synteny-selected paralog pairs within the pea and <i>M. truncatula</i> genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The massive increase of Ty3-<i>gypsy</i>, and to a lesser extent Ty1-<i>copia</i>, LTR-retrotransposons accounts for most of the genome size differences between pea and <i>M. truncatula, Trifolium pratense L., L. japonicus, P. vulgaris</i> or <i>G. max</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2178">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227–239 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2181">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="De Vega, J. J. et al. Red clover (Trifolium pratense L.) draft genome provides a platform for trait improvement. Sci. Rep. 5, 17394 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2184">35</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">10</a>). Investigation of TE representation in <i>Pisum</i> species and subspecies confirmed that TE dynamics has shaped <i>Pisum</i> diversity through successive expansions and deletions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig3">3a</a> and Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">6</a>). <i>P. fulvum</i> has fewer of several retroelements compared to cultivated pea and an increased content of <i>Ogre</i> retroelements. Wild <i>P. s. elatius</i> TE representation is intermediate between <i>P. fulvum</i> and cultivated pea. To determine the historical dynamics of the different <i>Ty3-gypsy</i> and <i>Ty1-copia</i> retroelements in the pea genome, we analyzed the divergence of the reverse transcriptase (RT) and integrase (INT) sequences of different TE lineages, revealing different evolutionary patterns among lineages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig3">3b,c</a>). For example, Angela elements are all relatively young, consistent with either an intense and recent burst of insertion or a strong selection against Angela elements. This is in marked contrast to TatV elements, which are the most ancient (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig3">3b</a>). Interestingly, all TE lineages that showed significant representational differences among <i>Pisum</i> species and subspecies were, on average, older or of the same age as <i>Ogre</i> elements (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig3">3c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="TE evolution in the pea genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: TE evolution in the pea genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="689" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, TE representation in <i>P. fulvum, P. s. elatius, P. s. sativum</i> landraces and <i>P. s. sativum</i> cultivars (<i>x</i> axis). The <i>y</i> axis of the plot represents the abundance of selected retrotransposon families as measured by the number of reads mapping to a lineage-specific RT domain divided by the total number of reads that map to all RT domains and by the number of RT domains in the assembly, per million. Letters on each quartile box-and-whisker plot represent statistically different classes among the different groups of accessions (<i>n</i> = 3 <i>P. fulvum</i>, <i>n</i> = 7 <i>P. elatius</i>, <i>n</i> = 10 <i>P. s. sativum</i> landraces and <i>n</i> = 5 <i>P. s. sativum</i> cultivars, Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">6</a>). <b>b</b>, Neighbor-joining (NJ) trees were built from RT domain sequence similarities among different lineage-specific copies identified in the pea genome v.1a assembly. Deep branching revealed ancient expansion while flat branching is consistent with a recent burst of insertion activity. Red branches correspond to outgroup sequences <b>c</b>, The average age of TEs was revealed for the different lineages by the branching distribution in the NJ trees built from RT (light blue) and INT (yellow) protein domains. The vertical bar ca. branching time 0.10 indicates the peak of <i>Ogre</i> retroelement age distribution. Stars indicate families for which TE representation significantly varied among <i>Pisum</i> taxa (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">5</a>). The RT and INT columns give the number of RT and INT domains present in the pea genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Paleohistory of modern legume genomes</h3><p>To assess the paleohistory of modern legume genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Lee, C., Yu, D., Choi, H. K. &amp; Kim, R. W. Reconstruction of a composite comparative map composed of ten legume genomes. Genes Genom. 39, 111–119 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2326">36</a></sup>, we performed homology and synteny analyses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>) with representatives of the Galegoid (<i>P. sativum, L. japonicus</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>C. arietinum</i>) and Millettoid (<i>C. cajan</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>Vigna angularis</i> (Willid.) Ohwi &amp; H. Ohashi) clades, together with one diploid peanut relative (<i>Arachis duranensis</i> Krapov. &amp; W.C. Greg). Within the Galegoid subfamily, we identified 12,025 ancestral genes (that is, conserved between the four investigated species) defining an ancestral Galegoid karyotype (AGK) of eight conserved ancestral regions (CARs). The pea genome differentiated from this AGK through at least three chromosomal fissions, four fusions and a translocation between chromosomes Ps1 and Ps5. The genome of the closely related <i>M. truncatula</i> evolved through two fissions, two fusions and one translocation (between Mt4-Mt8 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kamphuis, L. G. et al. The Medicago truncatula reference accession A17 has an aberrant chromosomal configuration. New Phytol. 174, 299–303 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2365">37</a></sup>), Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">9</a>). The five Millettoid genomes had 12,387 ancestral genes, defining an ancestral Millettoid karyotype (AMK) of 16 CARs. We then compared AGK, AMK and <i>A. duranensis</i>, an outgroup of the Galegoid and Millettoid subfamilies and identified 25 CARs with 13,181 protogenes. Merging CARs sharing partial synteny between a subset of these extant Millettoid and Galegoid genomes elucidated the ancestral legume karyotype (ALK), consisting of a minimum of 19 proto-chromosomes. We propose a legume evolutionary scenario from the reconstructed ancestral karyotypes showing that the legume genomes have been massively rearranged during their evolution (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">11</a>). This approach delivered the first reconstruction of the Legume (ALK) as well as Galegoid (AGK) and Millettoid (AMK) subfamily ancestors and updated the publicly available catalog of paralogous and orthologous gene relationships between extant legume genomes (<a href="https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/synteny/legumes">https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/synteny/legumes</a>) for translational research on conserved agronomical traits.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Legume evolutionary history."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Legume evolutionary history.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="325" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Evolutionary scenario of modern legumes (pea, diploid peanut, lotus, barrel medic, chickpea, pigeonpea, soybean, common bean, mungbean and adzuki bean) from the reconstructed ancestors of the Galegoid (AGK) and Millettoid (AMK) subfamilies as well as the ancestral legume karyotype (ALK with brackets under the 25 CARs defining 19 proto-chromosomes). Duplication event is shown with a red dot and estimated speciation dates are indicated on tree branches. The modern genomes are illustrated at the bottom with different colors reflecting the origin from ALK (referenced as the ALK painting) or from the inferred Galegoid and Millettoid ancestors (referenced as the AGK and AMK painting).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Pisum genome structure evolution</h3><p>‘Caméor’ shows a translocation compared to the ancestral Galegoid karyotype and while translocations within <i>Pisum</i> have long been known<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hammarlund, C. &amp; Håkansson, A. Parallelism of chromosome ring formation, sterility and linkage in Pisum. Hereditas 14, 97–98 (1930)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2420">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sansome, E. Segmental interchange lines in Pisum sativum. Nature 139, 113 (1937)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2420_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Lamm, R. &amp; Miravalle, R. J. A translocation tester set in Pisum. Hereditas 45, 417–440 (1959)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2423">22</a></sup>, identifying the chromosomes involved suffered from the lack of clear chromosome identification. Cytological analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ben-Ze'ev, N. &amp; Zohary, D. Species relationships in the genus Pisum L. Isr. J. Bot. 22, 73–91 (1973)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2427">38</a></sup> identified pairwise crosses between (1) <i>P. sativum</i>, including northern <i>P. humile,</i> (2) <i>P. elatius</i>, including southern <i>P. humile</i> and (3) <i>P. fulvum</i>, which gave rise to chromosomal rings during F<sub>1</sub> meiosis and to low hybrid fertility, suggesting that chromosome translocations accompanied <i>Pisum</i> evolution. To reassess these events in the light of the pea genome assembly, we sequenced single-chromosome samples isolated from three accessions that were used by Ben-Ze’ev and Zohary<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ben-Ze'ev, N. &amp; Zohary, D. Species relationships in the genus Pisum L. Isr. J. Bot. 22, 73–91 (1973)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2452">38</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). These three lines were considered archetypes of wild species and subspecies: ‘703’ for <i>P. fulvum</i>, ‘721’ for <i>P. elatius</i> and ‘711’ for southern <i>P. humile</i>. DNA amplified from ~40 single chromosomes obtained for each (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">10</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">12</a>) was sequenced. Mapping reads from each chromosome sample to the ‘Caméor’ pseudomolecules identified the correspondence between the wild pea and Caméor chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig5">5a,b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">11</a>). All wild pea chromosomes were assigned to ‘Caméor’ chromosomes, but for accessions ‘711’, ‘721’ and ‘703’, reads from chromosome samples corresponding to pseudomolecule 5 mapped only from 0 to 465 Mb of this pseudomolecule and chromosome samples with reads mapping from ~465 Mb to the end of ‘Caméor’ pseudomolecule 5 also mapped to another ‘Caméor’ chromosome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig5">5b</a>). For accessions ‘711’ and ‘721’, these mapped predominantly to pseudomolecule 1 of ‘Caméor’, while for ‘703’ they mapped predominantly to pseudomolecule 3 of ‘Caméor’ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig5">5b</a>). This indicated a translocation between chromosomes 5 and 1 in ‘711’ and ‘721’ and between chromosomes 5 and 3 in ‘703’ as compared to ‘Caméor’. Investigating synteny between pea and other Galegoid species suggested that the ancestral <i>Pisum</i> karyotype resembled the present <i>P.elatius/humile</i> karyotype rather than the cultivated pea karyotype. Indeed, ‘Caméor’ chromosome 5 is syntenic with <i>M. truncatula</i> chromosome 3 from 0 to 467 Mb and with chromosome 2 of <i>M. truncatula</i> from 467 Mb to its end (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig4">4</a>). This breakpoint in synteny is close to the translocation point but lies 2 Mb closer to the centromere of chromosome 5. Similarly, a breakpoint in synteny between ‘Caméor’ chromosome 5 and <i>C. arietinum</i> chromosome 5 occurred at this translocation point, with the translocated fragment being syntenic with <i>C. arietinum</i> chromosome 1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig4">4</a>). <i>C. arietinum</i> chromosome 1 and <i>M. truncatula</i> chromosome 2 are syntenic with ‘Caméor’ chromosome 1 and the end of ‘Caméor’ chromosome 5. Considering the AGK reconstruction (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">9</a>), the ancestral <i>Pisum</i> chromosome 1 probably contained the translocated fragment (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig5">5c</a>), as in the <i>P. elatius/humile</i> karyotype. This ancestral chromosome would then have been involved in two independent rearrangements, with the end of chromosome 1 translocated to chromosome 3 in <i>P. fulvum</i> and to chromosome 5 in cultivated pea. What remains unsolved is what role, if any, this breakage may have played in <i>Pisum</i> evolution and adaptation. We note that the repetitive 5 S rRNA gene sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Neumann, P., Nouzová, M. &amp; Macas, J. Molecular and cytogenetic analysis of repetitive DNA in pea (Pisum sativum L.). Genome 44, 716–728 (2001)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2539">39</a></sup> are present at these chromosomal regions (end of chromosome 1, 3 and pericentromeric regions of chromosome 5) suggestive of a role in these translocations.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Pisum genome structure evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: <i>Pisum</i> genome structure evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1159" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Flow-sorted single chromosomes of ‘Caméor’ were resequenced and reads mapped onto pseudomolecules. The example shows the reads mapping of a ‘Caméor’ chromosome sample corresponding to pseudomolecule 5. The color-codes for chromosomes are as in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig1">1a</a>. <b>b</b>, Mapping reads of flow-sorted single chromosomes of accessions ‘703’ (<i>P. fulvum</i>), ‘711’ (<i>P. s. humile</i>) and ‘721’ (<i>P. s. elatius</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ben-Ze'ev, N. &amp; Zohary, D. Species relationships in the genus Pisum L. Isr. J. Bot. 22, 73–91 (1973)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2576">38</a></sup> identified the correspondence between wild pea chromosomes and the pea genome v.1a pseudomolecules. All chromosomes corresponded one to one to ‘Caméor’ chromosomes except for chromosome 5. Most of the short arm of chromosome 5 (depicted as gray boxes) was associated with other chromosomes in wild <i>Pisum</i> (chromosome 1 in <i>P. s. elatius</i> and <i>P. s. humile</i>, chromosome 3 in <i>P. fulvum</i>). <b>c</b>, Scenario of chromosome evolution. <i>M. truncatula</i> karyotype was used to infer the ancestral <i>Pisum</i> karyotype. In this scenario, two independent translocation events occurred, one leading to present <i>P. fulvum</i> and the other to <i>P. s. sativum</i> karyotypes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Pisum genetic diversity</h3><p><i>Pisum</i> is extremely diverse in terms of phenotypes, and pea breeding could benefit from broad crosses, including introgressions from wild relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ladizinsky, G. &amp; Abbo, S. (eds.) The Pisum genus. in The Search for Wild Relatives of Cool Season Legumes 55–68 (Springer, 2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2626">40</a></sup>. Reproductive barriers are not strict among <i>Pisum</i> species and subspecies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Kosterin, O. E. &amp; Bogdanova, V. S. Reciprocal compatibility within the genus Pisum L. as studied in F1 hybrids: 1. Crosses involving P. sativum L. subsp. sativum. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 62, 691–709 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2633">41</a></sup>. Davis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Davis, P. H. in Flora of Turkey and the East Aegean Islands Vol. 3 (ed P. H. Davis) 370–373 (Edinburgh Univ., 1970)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2637">42</a></sup> proposed that <i>Pisum</i> comprises two species, <i>P. fulvum</i> and <i>P. sativum</i>, with two subspecies: <i>P. s. sativum,</i> which includes all formerly distinguished cultivated types, and <i>P. s. elatius,</i> which includes all formerly distinguished wild types. Although useful, this classification does not clarify the relationships between wild and domesticated forms, or between former taxa. To help refine <i>Pisum</i> taxonomy and evolution, we resequenced the genomes of 36 <i>Pisum</i> accessions representing the range of diversity of the species and one <i>Lathyrus sativus</i> accession as an outgroup. We also included public data from seven <i>Pisum</i> accessions (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">7</a>). Because the boundary between wild and cultivated <i>Pisum</i> is blurred by possible introgressions and/or migration, we reassessed the ‘wild’ and ‘cultivated’ status of accessions by scoring germination after imbibing freshly harvested seeds in water for 7 d. Free germination is indeed considered the most important pea domestication trait<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ladizinsky, G. &amp; Abbo, S.
