(eds.) The Pisum genus. in The Search for Wild Relatives of Cool Season Legumes 55–68 (Springer, 2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2676">40</a></sup>. The accessions presented a wide range of phenotypic diversity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig6">6a</a>) as shown by principal component analysis (PCA) of plant morphology, phenology, seed productivity and quality traits, which separated wild, landrace and cultivar accessions (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="The genetic relationships among the Pisum genus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: The genetic relationships among the <i>Pisum</i> genus.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="761" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b>, PCA of phenotypic traits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>) discriminating the different <i>Pisum</i> gene pools. In green, modern cultivar accessions; in orange, landrace accessions; in burgundy, wild <i>Pisum elatius</i> and <i>humile</i> accessions; in brown, wild <i>Pisum</i> <i>fulvum</i> accessions, in purple<i>, P. abyssinicum</i> accessions<b>. b</b>, Alleles shared between wild, landrace and modern cultivar accessions. Resequencing data for 43 <i>Pisum</i> and a <i>Lathyrus</i> accessions detected 17.2 M high-quality SNPs, corresponding to 37.6 M alleles. <b>c</b>, Pisum phylogenetic tree was obtained using a subset of 2 M high-quality SNPs and taking <i>Lathyrus sativus</i> as an outgroup. All clades have &gt;95% support (1,000 bootstrap runs). Former <i>Pisum</i> subspecies nomenclature described groups of accessions. Dots on the right-hand side indicate the germination ability of freshly harvested seeds on water, a key trait in pea domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ladizinsky, G. &amp; Abbo, S. (eds.) The Pisum genus. in The Search for Wild Relatives of Cool Season Legumes 55–68 (Springer, 2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2746">40</a></sup>. The two <i>P. abyssinicum</i> accessions (*) are cultivated peas but were clustered among wild <i>Pisum</i> accessions.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Whole-genome resequencing reads were mapped onto the pea genome assembly and SNPs were called using BCFtools v.1.6. After filtering, 17,212,424 high-quality SNPs were identified. On 37,591,394 alleles, 51.6% were shared among wild, landrace and cultivar accessions, 25.6% were present only in wild accessions, 3.5% only in landraces and 0.5% only in cultivars (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig6">6b</a>). Mean nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) decreased 1.7-fold between wild accessions (<i>π</i> = 8.2 × 10<sup>−4</sup>) and landraces (<i>π</i> = 4.9 × 10<sup>−4</sup>), and 3.4-fold between wild accessions and cultivars (<i>π</i> = 2.4 × 10<sup>−4</sup>), showing moderate diversity reduction associated with pea domestication and breeding (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig6">6b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">12</a>). This reduction was accompanied by a high mean pairwise population differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) between wild accessions and cultivars (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> = 0.213) and an increase in linkage disequilibrium (LD) across the genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">13</a>). Mean D Tajima values were significantly positive in wild accessions (<i>D</i> = 0.424) and slightly negative in cultivars, consistent with recent selection (<i>D</i> = −0.038, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">12</a>). Phylogenetic analysis of a subset of two million SNPs clustered accessions according to assigned taxon (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig6">6c</a>): <i>P. fulvum</i> clustered separately from <i>P. sativum</i> accessions. <i>P. sativum</i> accessions clustered according to their cultivated status (wild or cultivated) as well as their geographical origin and usage type (that is, as fodder, dry or fresh seeds). Wild <i>P. s. elatius</i> included former <i>P. elatius</i> and <i>P. humile</i> and cultivated <i>P. s. sativum</i> included <i>P. transcaucasicum, P. asiaticum, P. arvense. P. hortense</i>, but not <i>Pisum abyssinicum</i>. The two <i>P. abyssinicum</i> accessions clustered among the wild <i>P. sativum elatius/humile</i> accessions from Israel while presenting phenotypic attributes of cultivated accessions, including free germination (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig6">6c</a>). This strengthens the hypothesis of an independent domestication of this taxon from a distinct <i>P. s. elatius</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Weeden, N. F. Domestication of pea (Pisum sativum L.): the case of the Abyssinicum pea. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 515 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2860">43</a></sup> followed by a migration to Abyssinia possibly through ancient human trading routes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Pagani, L. et al. Ethiopian genetic diversity reveals linguistic stratification and complex influences on the Ethiopian gene pool. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 91, 83–96 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2864">44</a></sup>. The chloroplast phylogenetic tree supports this scenario (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">14</a>). Notably, the <i>P. elatius</i> accession closest to the cultivated pea was PI639984, an accession collected in 1986 on an abandoned agricultural terrace in Turkey, within the area where pea cultivation emerged.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Seed storage protein gene families</h3><p>Pea is an important source of dietary proteins for humans and domestic animals. Fractionation of pea seeds into protein, starch and fiber is expanding rapidly in North America and Europe in response to the demand for plant-based protein. Pea seed storage proteins (SSPs) include legumin, vicilin and convicilin globulins and PA1 and PA2 albumins, whose nutritional and technological properties vary according to their amino-acid content and secondary structure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Gabriel, I. et al. Variation in seed protein digestion of different pea (Pisum sativum L.) genotypes by cecectomized broiler chickens: 1. Endogenous amino acid losses, true digestibility and in vitro hydrolysis of proteins. Livest. Sci. 113, 251–261 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2883">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Rubio, L. A. et al. Characterization of pea (Pisum sativum) seed protein fractions. J. Sci. Food Agric. 94, 280–287 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2886">46</a></sup>. We searched the pea genome assembly for SSP genes using all pea storage protein genes available in UNIPROT (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>) and found 12, 9, 2, 8 and 9 genes encoding legumin, vicilin, convicilin, PA1 and PA2, respectively, as well as a few pseudogenes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">8</a>).</p><p>The various SSPs that characterize the pea seed proteome vary in quantity in response to the environment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bourgeois, M. et al. Dissecting the proteome of pea mature seeds reveals the phenotypic plasticity of seed protein composition. Proteomics 9, 254–271 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2899">47</a></sup>. Their diversity is magnified by the range of (1) cleavage sites controlling pre-polypeptide cleavage (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">15</a>) and (2) transcriptional regulatory regions. Several regulatory motifs, upstream of the SSP genes are presumed to modulate their expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Casey, R. &amp; Domoney, C. in Seed Proteins (eds Shewry, P. R. &amp; Casey, R.) 171–208 (Kluwer Academic Publishers, 1999)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2906">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Yoshino, M., Nagamatsu, A., Tsutsumi, K. I. &amp; Kanazawa, A. The regulatory function of the upstream sequence of the β-conglycinin α subunit gene in seed-specific transcription is associated with the presence of the RY sequence. Genes Genet. Syst. 81, 135–141 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2909">49</a></sup> (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">8</a>) dependent on developmental and environmental cues. The RY motif, reported to be required for SSP seed expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Yamamoto, S., Nishihara, M., Morikawa, H., Yamauchi, D. &amp; Minamikawa, T. Promoter analysis of seed storage protein genes from Canavalia gladiata DC. Plant Mol. Biol. 27, 729–741 (1995)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2916">50</a></sup>, was found upstream of all but three SSP genes, with some having seven upstream RY motifs. Other motifs were found upstream legumin genes (for example ABRE motif) or vicilin genes (for example ACGT motif). Expression analysis of some SSP genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig7">7a</a> and Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">8</a>), assessed by microfluidic quantitative PCR, showed that RY motifs were not systematically associated with seed specific expression. Examination of <i>Legumin</i> and <i>Vicilin</i> genes in pea and <i>M. truncatula</i> showed an overall conservation of tandem organization in these two species: clusters of SSP genes were found on syntenic pea and <i>M. truncatula</i> chromosomes, but gene copy number differed (<i>Vicilin</i> and <i>Legumin</i> genes on syntenic Ps3 and Mt7, <i>Convicilin</i> and <i>Legumin</i> genes on syntenic Ps6 and Mt1). Additional gene clusters were found in pea (<i>Vicilin</i> genes on Ps5 and <i>Legumin</i> genes on Ps6 and Ps4, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig7">7b</a>). Interestingly, all <i>Legumin</i> and <i>Vicilin</i> gene cluster positions in pea corresponded to reported SSP quantity loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Bourgeois, M. et al. A PQL (protein quantity loci) analysis of mature pea seed proteins identifies loci determining seed protein composition. Proteomics 11, 1581–1594 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d37755e2968">51</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Pea seed storage protein gene families."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: Pea seed storage protein gene families.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0480-1/MediaObjects/41588_2019_480_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="712" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <i>Legumin</i> gene tree including sequences from the pea genome reference, the UniProt database and the pea gene atlas reveals different clusters distributed on four loci; gene expression in developing seeds was investigated by microfluidic quantitative PCR using specific primers and is shown as color-coded bars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). Expression levels were averaged over three biological replicates. <b>b</b>, Organization of genes encoding globulins in the pea and the <i>M. truncatula</i> genomes reveal some conserved features. Chromosome color-codes are as in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig4">4</a> showing the syntenic relationships between the pea and <i>M. truncatula</i> genomes. Figures above chromosome bars indicate the size of each chromosome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Pea is an important plant-based protein source for human food and animal feed. This reference genome provides a foundation to elucidate <i>Pisum</i> evolution. The <i>Pisum</i> common ancestor was probably cytogenetically like <i>P. s. elatius</i>, this taxon evolved across the Mediterranean and Middle East<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ladizinsky, G. &amp; Abbo, S. (eds.) The Pisum genus. in The Search for Wild Relatives of Cool Season Legumes 55–68 (Springer, 2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3031">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Smýkal, P. et al. Genomic diversity and macroecology of the crop wild relatives of domesticated pea. Sci. Rep. 7, 17384 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3034">52</a></sup> and gave rise in the northern Middle East to <i>P. s. sativum</i>. <i>P. fulvum</i> diverged from the <i>Pisum</i> ancestor in the southern Middle East. <i>P. abyssinicum</i>, an Ethiopian cultivated form, is likely the result of a domestication event from a southern <i>P. s. elatius</i> ancestor and is independent of the domestication of <i>P. s. sativum</i>. Different lines of evidence suggested that the pea genome is evolving at a faster pace than other investigated Leguminosae genomes, potentially through transposon-mediated unequal recombination giving rise to gain or loss of genes, or ectopic double-strand break repair<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, S. F. et al. Chromosome evolution in connection with repetitive sequences and epigenetics in plants. Genes 8, 290 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3057">34</a></sup>. Differential expansion and removal of these elements probably shaped genomes throughout the evolution of the <i>Fabeae</i> and notably within <i>Pisum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Macas, J. et al. In depth characterization of repetitive DNA in 23 plant genomes reveals sources of genome size variation in the legume tribe Fabeae. PLoS One 10, e0143424 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3067">19</a></sup>, suggesting that repetitive elements were major drivers in the evolution of these large genomes. A valuable tool for basic discovery, this high-quality, annotated pea genome sequence will facilitate the characterization of its many known mutants, enhance pea improvement and allow more efficient use of the wide genetic diversity present in the genus.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome sequencing</h3><p>To enable an optimal assembly of the large (~4.45 Gb) and complex (85% repetitive DNA) pea reference genome, more than 1,300 Gb sequence data (equivalent to 294-fold genome coverage) were generated using DNA extracted from fresh plant material of the French pea cultivar ‘Caméor’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). The data included 100 and 150 bp Illumina reads and PacBio RSII read batches, one N50 = 9,500 kb and another N50 = 15,917 kb. The Illumina reads derived from paired-end libraries with insert sizes of 300, 500, 600 and 800 bp and ten mate-pair libraries with insert sizes between 3 and 17 kb. All sequence data have been deposited in EBI Bioproject <a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB30482">PRJEB30482</a> (Illumina reads) and NCBI Bioproject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA509681">PRJNA509681</a> (PacBio reads). Reads 150 bp long, 30-fold genome coverage equivalent, were randomly sampled and genome size was estimated using the GenomeScope program (<a href="http://qb.cshl.edu/genomescope/">http://qb.cshl.edu/genomescope/</a>). The estimated genome size of ‘Caméor’ through this method (4.426 Gb) was consistent with previous estimates obtained by flow-cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Doležel, J. et al. Plant genome size estimation by flow cytometry: inter-laboratory comparison. Ann. Bot. 82, 17–26 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3107">9</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">De novo assembly</h3><p>The pea nuclear genome was assembled into seven pseudomolecules in a step-wise manner. The assembly pipeline is summarized in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">2</a>. Shotgun Illumina reads were assembled using SoapdeNovo2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3122">53</a></sup>) with 127 nt <i>K</i>-mer and the –R option in the ‘pregraph’ step. Contigs shorter than 500 nt were removed. The remaining contigs were scaffolded with SSPACE 2.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3129">54</a></sup>) using the information captured by mate-pair reads; scaffolds 2 kb or larger, and validated by at least five read pairs, were considered as part of a first draft assembly. This assembly was improved with layers of data from physical maps (Whole-Genome Profiling, WGP), optical maps (Bionano maps), various high-density linkage maps (Genetic maps) and synteny to the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome. The physical map was produced using 295,680 BAC clones of cv. ‘Caméor’ pooled in a multi-dimensional manner. The BAC library was provided by INRA IPS2 and is available at INRA CNRGV (<a href="https://cnrgv.toulouse.inra.fr/fr/Banques/Pois">https://cnrgv.toulouse.inra.fr/fr/Banques/Pois</a>); its average BAC insert size is 125 kb and its genome coverage is 9.3×. The BAC DNA was digested with <i>Hin</i>dIII/<i>Mse</i>I; fragments were ligated, amplified by PCR, and sequenced using Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform (100 nt read length). The reads were clustered according to the parental BAC clones’ ID and assembled using FPC software (Keygene N.V.). The physical map was generated according to Gali et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Gali, K. K. et al. Development of a sequence-based reference physical map of pea (Pisum sativum L.). Front. Plant Sci. 10, 323 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3150">23</a></sup> and used to link the scaffolds in the draft assembly into super-scaffolds using MaGuS 1.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Madoui, M.-A. et al. MaGuS: a tool for quality assessment and scaffolding of genome assemblies with whole genome profilingTM Data. BMC Bioinformatics 17, 115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3154">55</a></sup>) and the WGP technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="van Oeveren, J. et al. Sequence-based physical mapping of complex genomes by whole genome rofiling. Genome Res. 21, 618–625 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3158">56</a></sup> (Keygene N.V.). Gaps in super-scaffolds were closed with GapCloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3163">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3166">58</a></sup> using paired-end, mate-pair and PacBio reads. Super-scaffolds were manually curated for inter and intrachromosomal chimeras (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary notes</a>) using (1) sequences obtained from single chromosomes isolated by flow-cytometry sorting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Neumann, P., Pozárková, D., Vrána, J., Doležel, J. &amp; Macas, J. Chromosome sorting and PCR-based physical mapping in pea (Pisum sativum L.). Chromosome Res. 10, 63–71 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3173">24</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">3</a>, Bioprojects at ENA <a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB30482">PRJEB30482</a>, and at NCBI <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA507688">PRJNA507688</a>) and (2) an ultra-high-density genetic map obtained from 162 RILs derived from the cross between ‘Caméor’ × ‘Melrose’ (Pop6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tayeh, N. et al. Development of two major resources for pea genomics: the GenoPea 13.2K SNP Array and a high density, high resolution consensus genetic map. Plant J. 84, 1257–1273 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3195">25</a></sup> and genotyped by skim genotyping-by-sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Bayer, P. E. et al. High-resolution skim genotyping by sequencing reveals the distribution of crossovers and gene conversions in Cicer arietinum and Brassica napus. Theor. Appl. Genet. 128, 1039–1047 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3199">59</a></sup> (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">1</a>, Bioproject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA507685">PRJNA507685</a>), it is worth noting this map included 468,448 SNPs and represents the highest density genetic map published for pea. Manually corrected scaffolds were integrated into 24,623 super-scaffolds (L50 of 415 Kb; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">2</a>) using an optical map generated from ‘Caméor’ high-molecular weight DNA prepared from the nuclei of young leaves following the IrysPrep protocols (BioNano Genomics; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">3</a>). The curated super-scaffolds were anchored onto high-density genetic maps (derived from populations Pop4, 5, 7, 9 described by Tayeh et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tayeh, N. et al. Development of two major resources for pea genomics: the GenoPea 13.2K SNP Array and a high density, high resolution consensus genetic map. Plant J. 84, 1257–1273 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3220">25</a></sup>, and Pop6’s map described herein) using Allmaps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3224">60</a></sup> to form quasi-chromosomal pseudomolecules. The genome of the model legume <i>M. truncatula</i> v.4 (JCVI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Tang, H. et al. An improved genome release (version Mt4.0) for the model legume Medicago truncatula. BMC Genomics 27, 312 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3231">61</a></sup>) was used for scaffold orientation when no indication from pea genetic map. The assembly, the pea genome v.1a, is available at <a href="https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Species/Pisum">https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Species/Pisum</a> and at the European Nucleotide Archive (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/">http://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/</a>) under project <a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB31320">PRJEB31320</a>. A genome JBrowse is available at <a href="https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Species/Pisum">https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Species/Pisum</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome annotation: repetitive sequences, gene models and microRNAs</h3><p>The REPET package v.2.6 (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Flutre, T., Duprat, E., Feuillet, C. &amp; Quesneville, H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PloS One 6, e16526 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3272">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Quesneville, H. et al. Combined evidence annotation of transposable elements in genome sequences. PLoS Comput. Biol. 1, e22 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3275">63</a></sup>) was used to identify and annotate repetitive elements in contigs of the pea genome sequence as summarized in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">6</a>. A sample from each pseudomolecule, consisting of 700 Mb of the longest scaffolds, served to compare the genome to itself using the pipeline TEdenovo to detect repeats present in at least three copies; 200 Mb were aligned to themselves to identify repeats and RepeatScout was applied to screen the remaining 500 Mb for repetitive low complexity DNA. Identified repeat sequences were clustered by multiple alignments to produce a library of consensus sequences. The repeat consensus sequences were classified according to their characteristics and redundancy using PASTEC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Hoede, C. et al. PASTEC: an automatic transposable element classification tool. PLoS ONE 9, e91929 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3282">64</a></sup> with Repbase (v.20.05). TEannot then mapped the repeat consensus sequence library produced by TEdenovo against the genome using a two-step approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Jamilloux, V., Daron, J., Choulet, F. &amp; Quesneville, H. De novo annotation of transposable elements: tackling the fat genome issue. Proc. IEEE 105, 474–481 (2107)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3286">65</a></sup>. The first step identified consensus sequences with at least one full-copy fragment in the genome. The second step identified the copies of these elements in the genome. The annotation of transposon-protein-domains was refined using DANTE (RepeatExplorer server; <a href="https://repeatexplorer-elixir.cerit-sc.cz/">https://repeatexplorer-elixir.cerit-sc.cz/</a>) against a custom database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Novák, P., Neumann, P. &amp; Macas, J. Graph-based clustering and characterization of repetitive sequences in next-generation sequencing data. BMC Bioinformatics 11, 378 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3298">66</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Novák, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792–793 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3301">67</a></sup>. The hits were filtered to cover at least 80% of the reference sequence, minimum identity of 35% and minimum similarity of 45%, allowing for a maximum of three interruptions (frameshifts or stop codons). TE classes were defined according to Wicker et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3305">68</a></sup> and TE lineages were defined according to Novak et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Novák, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792–793 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3309">67</a></sup>. The density of TE consensus copies according to their lineages were computed along pseudomolecules and visualized using 1 Mb windows each 500 kb step (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#Fig1">1</a>). The identification and quantification of repetitive sequences from unassembled Illumina reads were done using RepeatExplorer. The pipeline was run with default parameters, using 3,972,596 paired-end reads (100 nt) as input.</p><p>Gene models were predicted de novo using AUGUSTUS v.3.0.3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Keller, O. et al. A novel hybrid gene prediction method employing protein multiple sequence alignments. Bioinformatics 6, 757–763 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3319">69</a></sup>) and Fgenesh v.7.1.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Solovyev, V. et al. Automatic annotation of eukaryotic genes, pseudogenes and promoters. Genome Biol. 7, S10 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3323">70</a></sup>) trained on the <i>M. truncatula</i> gene matrix once repetitive DNA was masked using <i>maskfasta</i> v.5.1.22. Protein homology searches (TBLASTN) were done using sequences from: (1) <i>C. arietinum</i> (GA_v.1.0), <i>G. max</i> (275_Wm82.a2.v.1), <i>M. truncatula</i> (Mt4.0 v.1) retaining hits with an <i>E</i> value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−50</sup> and more than 50% of the protein length mapped; (2) UniProt and Swissprot databases retaining hits with an <i>E</i>-value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>–20</sup>; (3) pea DNA and RNA sequences from IPK and NCBI retaining hits with an <i>E</i> value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>–50</sup> and identity criteria ≥98%. Retained sequences were analyzed using Exonerate v.2.2.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3359">71</a></sup>) to generate protein-based gene models. To refine the annotation and identify splice junctions, RNA-seq reads from a series of libraries were aligned to the genome assembly using the ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner STAR (v.STAR_2.4.0j<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15–21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3363">72</a></sup>: Twenty RNA-seq libraries from various plant tissues of ‘Caméor’ at different plant growth stages (188,446,568 reads) are described in Alves-Carvalho et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Alves-Carvalho, S. Full-length de novo assembly of RNA-seq data in pea (Pisum sativum L.) provides a gene expression atlas and gives insights into root nodulation in this species. Plant J. 84, 1–19 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3367">73</a></sup> and 12 highly dense libraries generated from cultivar Kaspa inoculated with isolates of the fungal complex causing Ascochyta blight and mock-inoculated leaf tissue (160,332,071 reads) are described by Turo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Turo, C. J. Genomic Analysis of Fungal Species Causing Ascochyta Blight in Field Pea. PhD thesis, Curtin Univ. (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3371">74</a></sup> and available in NCBI Bioproject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA510273">PRJNA510273</a>. A set of assembled transcripts were obtained from the alignments using StringTie (v.1.2.2) (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotech. 33, 290 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3383">75</a></sup>) and Trinity-GG (v.2.0.6) (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3387">76</a></sup>). Integration of all above gene models and identification of alternative splice sites were done using the annotation pipeline PASA v.2.0.2, which includes Evidence Modeler v.1.1.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3391">77</a></sup>). The completeness of the gene repertoire was assessed using BUSCO v.3.0.2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3395">78</a></sup>).</p><p>Putative gene functions were assigned using the best match to SwissProt and TrEMBL databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="The UniProt Consortium. Ongoing and future developments at the Universal Protein Resource. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D214–D219 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3402">79</a></sup>. Motifs and domains were searched using InterProScan v.5 (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3406">80</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Cock, P. J. A., Grüning, B. A., Paszkiewicz, K. &amp; Pritchard, L. Galaxy tools and workflows for sequence analysis with applications in molecular plant pathology. Peer J. 1, e167 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3409">81</a></sup>) against all default protein databases including ProDom, PRINTS, PfamA, SMART, TIGRFAM, PrositeProfiles, HAMAP, PrositePatterns, SITE, SignalP, TMHMM, Panther, Gene3d, Phobius, Coils and CDD. In addition, we used TrapID (<a href="http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/webtools/trapid/">http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/webtools/trapid/</a>), and the PLAZA v.2.5 reference database to assign each transcript to a reference gene family and transfer functional annotation including GO for each transcript. Additionally, an embedded pipeline of EuGene v.4.2 (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Foissac, S. et al. Genome annotation in plants and fungi: EuGene as a model platform. Curr. Bioinf. 3, 87–97 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3420">82</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3423">83</a></sup>)was launched using the same proteins and RNA-seq databases. This annotation procedure yielded 34,137 gene models and was used to curate gene models manually.</p><p>For the identification of miRNA, developing seeds of ‘Caméor’ were harvested at two stages (12 d and 22 d after pollination). RNA was purified and small RNA libraries were produced and sequenced according to Lelandais-Briere et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Lelandais-Brière, C. et al. Genome-wide Medicago truncatula small RNA analysis revealed novel microRNAs and isoforms differentially regulated in roots and nodules. Plant Cell 21, 2780–2796 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3430">84</a></sup>. Reads were pooled, trimmed using fastx clipper and a minimum length of 15 nt, and mapped to identify miRNA using ShortStacks (v.3.8.5). ShortStacks classify putative miRNA following several criteria: Y miRNA classification indicates that the miRNA sequence passed all tests including sequencing of the exact miRNA-star, supporting a de novo annotation of a new miRNA family. N15 miRNA classification indicates that the miRNA sequence passed all tests except that the miRNA-star was not sequenced. Y and N15 miRNA were mapped against miRbase v.22 mature miRNA sequences using ssearch36, and only alignment with at least 95% of identity were conserved. For N15 miRNAs, only those with a match to a known plant miRNA were kept. Y miRNAs without annotation were considered newly identified miRNA. Finally, targets were predicted using TargetFinder and kept only if their score was greater than 3. Fifty-nine miRNAs showed at least one putative target (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">3b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome structure and evolution</h3><p>To identify putative paralogous and orthologous gene clusters, protein-coding genes sets from pea and 21 other eudicot species (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">4</a>) were analyzed using Orthofinder v.2.1.2 and its defaults parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3448">85</a></sup> with the Diamond v.0.9.14 option instead of BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Buchfink, B., Xie, C. &amp; Huson, D. H. Fast and sensitive protein alignment using DIAMOND. Nat. Methods 12, 59–60 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3452">86</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). Before the analysis, genome assemblies and annotations were subjected to minor amendments to exclude plastid sequence data, inconsistencies in the headings format between fasta and gff3 files, spurious stop codons or sequences with premature stop codons and alternative transcripts. In cases where there were two or more transcript variants, the longest transcript was selected to represent the coding region (input data is summarized in Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">4</a>). The sequence divergence for all possible pairs of paralogs within each orthogroup was estimated based on pairwise Ks. Protein sequences were aligned using MUSCLE v.3.8.31 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3463">87</a></sup>) and converted into codon aligned nucleotides using the bioruby-alignment package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Bonnal, R. J. P. et al. Biogem: an effective tool-based approach for scaling up open source software development in bioinformatics. Bioinformatics 28, 1035–1037 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3467">88</a></sup>. Ks values were calculated through maximum likelihood estimation (MLE) using the ‘codeml’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Goldman, N. &amp; Yang, Z. A codon-based model of nucleotide substitution for protein-coding DNA sequences. Mol. Biol. Evol. 11, 725–736 (1994)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3471">89</a></sup> and ‘yn00’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Yang, Z. &amp; Nielsen, R. Estimating synonymous and nonsynonymous substitution rates under realistic evolutionary models. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 32–43 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3475">90</a></sup> programs in the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3479">91</a></sup> and using the following parameters: runmode = −2, set-type = 1 (codon sequences), alpha fixed to 0, codonFreq = 2 (F2X4). For that purpose, we created an in-memory sqlite database including the whole-genome assemblies and annotations to identify pairs of paralogs based on the Orthogroups.csv file. For all Ks distribution histograms, the <i>x</i> axes were drawn on a log-scale with non-transformed Ks values to represent the decreasing relative importance of differences as the Ks value increases resulting from the stochastic nature and saturation of Ks calculations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Vanneste, K., de Peer, Van &amp; Maere, Y. S. Inference of genome duplications from age distributions revisited. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 177–190 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3487">92</a></sup>. The range of values, 0.01–50, were binned into 400 interval-bins. To reduce the exponential effect of spurious homologs on background noise, we filtered the data based on orthogroup size. The histograms in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">7</a> represent paralogs pairs in orthogroups of 8 to 20 genes or less: for each species, the orthogroup size was determined based on the genome multiples for events leading to the eudicot divergence onwards (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">4</a>).</p><p>Based on both homology and synteny, we further investigated the paleohistory of legume genomes. An evolutionary scenario was obtained following the method described in Pont et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Pont, C. et al. Paleogenomics: reconstruction of plant evolutionary trajectories from modern and ancient DNA. Genome Biol. 20, 29 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3500">93</a></sup> based on synteny relationships identified between between pea (<i>P. sativum</i>), peanut diploid ancestor (<i>Arachis duranensis</i>,<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 47, 438–446 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3510">94</a></sup>), lotus (<i>Lotus japonicus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227–239 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3516">11</a></sup>), barrel medic (<i>Medicago truncatula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3523">10</a></sup>), chickpea (<i>Cicer arietinum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotech. 31, 240–246 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3529">95</a></sup>), pigeonpea (<i>Cajanus cajan</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Singh, N. K. et al. The first draft of the pigeonpea genome sequence. J. Plant Biochem. Biotechnol. 21, 98–112 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3535">96</a></sup>), soybean (<i>Glycine max</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3541">12</a></sup>), common bean (<i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat. Genet. 46, 707–713 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3548">97</a></sup>), mungbean (<i>Vigna radiata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Kang, Y. J. et al. Genome sequence of mungbean and insights into evolution within Vigna species. Nat. Commun. 5, 5443 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3554">98</a></sup>) and adzuki bean (<i>Vigna angularis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Kang, Y. J. et al. Draft genome sequence of adzuki bean Vigna angularis. Sci. Rep. 5, 8069 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3560">99</a></sup>). Genomes were aligned to define conserved or duplicated gene pairs based on alignment parameters, groups of conserved genes were clustered or chained into synteny blocks (excluding blocks with less than five genes) corresponding to independent sets of blocks sharing orthologous relationships in modern species. Then, conserved groups of gene-to-gene adjacencies defining identical chromosome-to-chromosome relationships between all the extant genomes were merged into CARs. CARs were merged into protochromosomes based on partial synteny observed between a subset of the investigated species. The ancestral karyotype is a ‘median’ or ‘intermediate’ genome consisting of proto-chromosomes defining a clean reference gene order, common to the extant species investigated. From the reconstructed ancestral karyotype an evolutionary scenario was then inferred taking into account the fewest number of genomic rearrangements, which may have occurred between the inferred ancestors and the modern genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Pisum diversity</h3><p>Genomic resequencing data of 44 accessions were used to study the pea genome diversity (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">7</a>). Sixteen genotypes, including Caméor, were resequenced as described in Tayeh et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tayeh, N. et al. Development of two major resources for pea genomics: the GenoPea 13.2K SNP Array and a high density, high resolution consensus genetic map. Plant J. 84, 1257–1273 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3579">25</a></sup>, as part of the ANR program GENOPEA (Bioproject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA285605">PRJNA285605</a>). Another 16 genotypes were chosen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tayeh, N. et al. Development of two major resources for pea genomics: the GenoPea 13.2K SNP Array and a high density, high resolution consensus genetic map. Plant J. 84, 1257–1273 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3590">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Smýkal, P. et al. Genomic diversity and macroecology of the crop wild relatives of domesticated pea. Sci. Rep. 7, 17384 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3593">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Siol, M. et al. Patterns of genetic structure and linkage disequilibrium in a large collection of pea germplasm. G3: Genes, Genomes, Genet. 7, 2461–2471 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3596">100</a></sup> and resequenced in the Pisdom Burgundy region PARI project (FABER M. Siol, Bioproject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA431567">PRJNA431567</a>). Nuclear DNA was extracted using the Floraclean Plant DNA isolation kit as recommended by MP Biomedicals (<a href="http://www.mpbio.com">http:/www.mpbio.com</a>). A quality control was performed for all DNA samples with Quant-iT PicoGreen (Invitrogen) and by measuring absorbance and checking electrophoretic profile on agarose gel. Illumina paired-end shotgun indexed libraries were prepared from one µg of DNA per genotype, using the TruSeq DNA PCR-free LT Sample Preparation Kit (Illumina Inc., <a href="https://www.illumina.com/">https://www.illumina.com/</a>). Paired-end sequencing 2 × 100 sequencing by synthesis (SBS) cycles was performed on a HiSeq 2000, TruSeq V.3 chemistry according to manufacturer’s instructions. Additionally, three genotypes (DSP, 90–2131, Kiflica; Bioproject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA509279">PRJNA509279</a>) were sequenced by a commercial company (NovoGene) using Illumina HiSeq, paired-end 150 bp from 350 bp insert DNA libraries and three accessions (‘703’, ‘711’, ‘721’) were resequenced at GENOSCOPE on an HiSeq2500 using the Nextera Mate Pair Sample preparation kit of Illumina (Bioproject <a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB30482">PRJEB30482</a>) as described above for the genome sequencing. All pea resequenced genotypes, except Zhongwan6 for which we had no seeds, were evaluated in the glasshouse for classical growth and development traits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a> and Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">1</a>). Two pots per accessions and six seeds per pot were sown in February 2017 in 7 l pots. In total, 59 phenotypic traits were scored on the 44 genotypes, including seed protein composition traits. Germination tests were conducted on freshly harvested seeds (five seeds per accession, three replicates) and mean germination rates were calculated.</p><p>Resequencing data for the 43 accessions of <i>Pisum</i> and the accession of <i>Lathyrus sativus</i> were mapped onto the pea genome v.1a assembly using BWA MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3651">101</a></sup>, keeping only unique mapping with a quality higher or equal to 30. Optical duplicates were removed with PICARD tools (<a href="http://picard.sourceforge.net/">http://picard.sourceforge.net/</a>). Altogether, 95,326,251 SNPs were called using BCFtools v.1.6 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3662">101</a></sup>) mpileup and call. All callings supported by less than three reads were reimputed. All markers that were homozygous or heterozygous in ‘Caméor’ as compared to the reference were deleted using SNPSift<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Cingolani, P. et al. Using Drosophila melanogaster as a model for genotoxic chemical mutational studies with a new program, SnpSift. Front. Genet. 3, 35 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3667">102</a></sup>. We produced two different datasets depending on the type of analysis to be conducted. For phylogenetic analysis, 2,026,659 SNPs with less than five missing data and ten heterozygotes were filtered using vcftools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3671">103</a></sup> and plink<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: A Tool Set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 559–575 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3675">104</a></sup> (Phylogeny SNP dataset). For diversity analysis, 17,212,608 SNPs with less than ten missing data and ten heterozygotes were filtered (Diversity SNP dataset). In this dataset, accessions L180 and Zhongwan6 were removed.</p><p>The ‘Phylogeny’ SNP dataset was used to build a phylogenetic tree of the 44 accessions using IQ-Tree v.1.6 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Nguyen, L. T. et al. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3682">105</a></sup>). TVM + R10 was selected as the best model for a maximum likelihood tree using Modelfinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Kalyaanamoorthy, S. et al. ModelFinder: fast model selection for accurate phylogenetic estimates. Nat. Methods 14, 587–589 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3686">106</a></sup>. The tree was inferred with 1000 replicates of ultrafast likelihood bootstrap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Hoang, D. T. et al. UFBoot2: improving the ultrafast bootstrap approximation. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 518–522 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3690">107</a></sup> and SH-aLRT test to obtain bootstrap branch support values. The number of alleles present in the different <i>Pisum</i> groups were computed using the ‘Diversity’ dataset. An in-house script was used to transform SNP information into alleles coded in an allele dose 012 format. The VennCounts function of the R package limma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Ritchie, M. E. et al. limma powers differential expression analyses for RNA-sequencing and microarray studies. Nucleic Acids Res. 4, 3 e47 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3697">108</a></sup> was used to calculate Venn diagrams for each group.</p><p>Resequencing reads obtained for wild, landrace and a few cultivar accessions were mapped on the genome using NGM by default<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Sedlazeck, F. J., Rescheneder, P. &amp; Von Haeseler, A. NextGenMap: fast and accurate read mapping in highly polymorphic genomes. Bioinformatics 29, 2790–2791 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3704">109</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). Counts were computed using FeatureCounts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Liao, Y., Smyth, G. K. &amp; Shi, W. featureCounts: an efficient general purpose program for assigning sequence reads to genomic features. Bioinformatics 30, 923–930 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3711">110</a></sup> on specific associated lineage domains. The reads mapping onto TE domains were counted and normalized by dividing the number of counts on a specific domain by the total number of counts on all TE domains and by the total number of occurrences of each domain in the pea genome v.1a assembly per million.</p><p>Statistical tests were performed as follows. The variation of TE representation among the different <i>Pisum</i> species and subspecies was tested using proc GLM (SAS Institute). Different models were tested by analysis of variance (ANOVA): Model1 tested the different TE representation between <i>P. fulvum, P. sativum</i> wild and <i>P. s. sativum</i> groups; Model 2, between <i>P. fulvum, P. sativum</i> wild, <i>P.sativum</i> landraces and <i>P.sativum</i> cultivars; and Model 3 between <i>P. fulvum, P. sativum</i> wild, <i>P. abyssinicum, P. sativum</i> landraces and <i>P.sativum</i> cultivars. Counts were normalized by dividing the number of counts on a specific domain by the total number of counts on all TE domains and by the total number of occurrence of each domain in the pea genome v.1a assembly per million. For Model 2, mean least square predicted values of normalized mapped reads’ count and their standard deviations were computed and two-tailed t-tests were performed for eight selected TE lineages.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Translocation analyses</h3><p>To identify chromosome translocations, we sequenced single chromosomes isolated by flow sorting from the three accessions <i>P. fulvum</i> ‘703’, <i>P. sativum elatius</i> ‘721’ and <i>P. sativum southern humile</i> ‘711’ characterized by Ben-Ze’ev and Zohary<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ben-Ze'ev, N. &amp; Zohary, D. Species relationships in the genus Pisum L. Isr. J. Bot. 22, 73–91 (1973)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3764">38</a></sup> and compared the sequences with the sequence assembly of <i>P. sativum</i> cv. Caméor. Preparation of suspensions of intact mitotic chromosomes, flow cytometric analysis and sorting was done according to Neumann et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Neumann, P., Pozárková, D., Vrána, J., Doležel, J. &amp; Macas, J. Chromosome sorting and PCR-based physical mapping in pea (Pisum sativum L.). Chromosome Res. 10, 63–71 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3772">24</a></sup>. For each genotype, 84 chromosomes were flow-sorted and single-chromosome DNA amplification was done (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). Of these, a total of 137 DNA samples were selected and sequenced (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). To identify the pseudomolecule that each sample corresponded to, we mapped the chromosome sequence data onto the genome assembly of <i>P. sativum</i> cv. Caméor. This identified the correspondence between chromosome samples and pseudomolecules.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Seed storage proteins annotation</h3><p>A list of storage protein sequences was set up by combining sequences retrieved from the pea gene atlas, UNIPROT and NCBI and searched for homologies in the pea genome assembly (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">4</a>). Candidate sequences were manually curated using protein alignments, RNA-seq data and gene models by euGene. Known regulatory motifs were searched in the 5′ region of the identified gene models (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">4</a>). Best homology matches were search for in Uniprot Genbank and the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome v.4. To assess seed storage protein gene expression, total RNA from seeds was extracted using an RNeasy plant mini kit (Qiagen, <a href="http://www.qiagen.com">www.qiagen.com</a>) after grinding plant tissue in liquid nitrogen using a pestle and mortar. cDNA were prepared according to Gallardo et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Gallardo, K. et al. A combined proteome and transcriptome analysis of developing Medicago truncatula seeds evidence for metabolic specialization of maternal and filial tissues. Mol. Cell. Proteomics 6, 2165–2179 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3809">111</a></sup>. Other cDNAs were produced as described in Alves-Carvalho et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Alves-Carvalho, S. Full-length de novo assembly of RNA-seq data in pea (Pisum sativum L.) provides a gene expression atlas and gives insights into root nodulation in this species. Plant J. 84, 1–19 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3814">73</a></sup>. High-throughput real-time quantitative PCR was performed using the Biomark microfluidic system from Fluidigm according to manufacturer’s protocol. Primers used are listed in Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM3">4</a>. Expression was normalized as in Alves-Carvalho et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Alves-Carvalho, S. Full-length de novo assembly of RNA-seq data in pea (Pisum sativum L.) provides a gene expression atlas and gives insights into root nodulation in this species. Plant J. 84, 1–19 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d37755e3821">73</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0480-1#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>All raw sequencing data are available at the European Nucleotide Archive (<a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB30482">PRJEB30482</a>) and as an NCBI BioProject (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA507685">PRJNA507685</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA507688">PRJNA507688</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA509681">PRJNA509681</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA510273">PRJNA510273</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA285605">PRJNA285605</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA431567">PRJNA431567</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA509279">PRJNA509279</a>). The pea genome v.1a reference assembly is available for download and JBrowse at <a href="https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Species/Pisum">https://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Species/Pisum</a>. The genome is also available at the European Nucleotide Archive under project <a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB31320">PRJEB31320</a>.</p>
